	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Burger King</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the restaurant chain. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Burger King (disambiguation)">Burger King (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><span style="word-spacing:0">This box: </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_Burger_King" title="Template:Infobox Burger King"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_Burger_King" title="Template talk:Infobox Burger King"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_Burger_King&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.35em;"><b>Footnotes&#160;/&#32;references</b><br /><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Burger King</b> (<b>BK</b>) is an American global <a href="/wiki/Chain_store" title="Chain store">chain</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburger</a> <a href="/wiki/Fast_food_restaurants" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast food restaurants">fast food restaurants</a>. Headquartered in the <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated area</a> of <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">Miami-Dade County, Florida</a>, the company was founded in 1953 as <b>Insta-Burger King</b>, a <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a>–based restaurant chain. After Insta-Burger King ran into financial difficulties in 1954, its two Miami-based franchisees <a href="/wiki/David_Edgerton" title="David Edgerton">David Edgerton</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_McLamore" title="James McLamore">James McLamore</a> purchased the company and renamed it "<b>Burger King</b>". Over the next half-century, the company would change hands four times, with its third set of owners, a partnership of <a href="/wiki/TPG_Capital" title="TPG Capital">TPG Capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bain_Capital" title="Bain Capital">Bain Capital</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Capital_Partners" title="Goldman Sachs Capital Partners">Goldman Sachs Capital Partners</a>, taking it public in 2002. In late 2010, <a href="/wiki/3G_Capital" title="3G Capital">3G Capital</a> of Brazil acquired a majority stake in the company, in a deal valued at US$3.26 billion. The new owners promptly initiated a restructuring of the company to reverse its fortunes. 3G, along with partner <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a>, eventually merged the company with the Canadian-based doughnut chain <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hortons" title="Tim Hortons">Tim Hortons</a>, under the auspices of a new Canadian-based parent company named <a href="/wiki/Restaurant_Brands_International" title="Restaurant Brands International">Restaurant Brands International</a>.
</p><p>The 1970s were the "Golden Age" of <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_advertising" title="Burger King advertising">the company's advertising</a>, but beginning in the early-1980s, Burger King advertising began losing focus. A series of less successful advertising campaigns created by a procession of advertising agencies continued for the next two decades. In 2003, Burger King hired the Miami-based advertising agency <a href="/wiki/Crispin_Porter_%2B_Bogusky" title="Crispin Porter + Bogusky">Crispin Porter + Bogusky</a> (CP+B), which completely reorganized its advertising with a series of new campaigns centered on a redesigned Burger King character nicknamed "The King", accompanied by a new online presence. While highly successful, some of CP+B's commercials were derided for perceived <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexism</a> or cultural insensitivity. Burger King's new owner, 3G Capital, later terminated the relationship with CP+B in 2011 and moved its advertising to <a href="/wiki/McGarryBowen" title="McGarryBowen">McGarryBowen</a>, to begin a new product-oriented campaign with expanded <a href="/wiki/Demographic_targeting" title="Demographic targeting">demographic targeting</a>.
</p><p>Burger King's <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_products" title="Burger King products">menu</a> has expanded from a basic offering of burgers, French fries, sodas, and milkshakes to a larger and more diverse set of products. In 1957, the "<a href="/wiki/Whopper" title="Whopper">Whopper</a>" became the first major addition to the menu, and it has become Burger King's signature product since. Conversely, BK has introduced many products which failed to catch hold in the marketplace. Some of these failures in the United States have seen success in foreign markets, where BK has also tailored its menu for regional tastes. From 2002 to 2010, Burger King aggressively targeted the 18–34 male demographic with larger products that often carried correspondingly large amounts of unhealthy fats and <a href="/wiki/Trans-fat" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans-fat">trans-fats</a>. This tactic would eventually damage the company's financial underpinnings, and cast a negative pall on its earnings. Beginning in 2011, the company began to move away from its previous male-oriented menu and introduce new menu items, product reformulations and packaging, as part of its current owner 3G Capital's restructuring plans of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of December 31, 2018, Burger King reported it had 17,796 outlets in 100 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-RESTAURANT_BRANDS_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RESTAURANT_BRANDS-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-largest_chains_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largest_chains-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Of these, nearly half are located in the United States, and 99.7% are <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_franchises" title="Burger King franchises">privately owned and operated</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-largest_chains_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largest_chains-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> with its new owners moving to an almost entirely franchised model in 2013. BK has historically used several variations of <a href="/wiki/Franchising" title="Franchising">franchising</a> to expand its operations. The manner in which the company licenses its franchisees varies depending on the region, with some regional franchises, known as <a href="/wiki/Master_franchise" title="Master franchise">master franchises</a>, responsible for selling franchise sub-licenses on the company's behalf. Burger King's relationship with its franchises has not always been harmonious. Occasional spats between the two have caused numerous issues, and in several instances, the company's and its licensees' relations have degenerated into precedent-setting court cases. Burger King's Australian franchise <a href="/wiki/Hungry_Jack%27s" title="Hungry Jack&#39;s">Hungry Jack's</a> is the only franchise to operate under a different name, due to a trademark dispute and a series of legal cases between the two.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Burger_King" title="History of Burger King">History of Burger King</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Original_Burger_king_Logo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Original_Burger_king_Logo.png/200px-Original_Burger_king_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="65" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Original_Burger_king_Logo.png/300px-Original_Burger_king_Logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Original_Burger_king_Logo.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="130" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Wordmark" title="Wordmark">Wordmark</a> used from 1954 until 1957</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The predecessor to Burger King was founded in 1953 in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a>, as Insta-Burger King.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_2012_p._66_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2012_p._66-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After visiting the <a href="/wiki/Richard_and_Maurice_McDonald" title="Richard and Maurice McDonald">McDonald brothers'</a> original store location in <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino,_California" title="San Bernardino, California">San Bernardino, California</a>, the founders and owners (Keith J. Kramer and his wife's uncle Matthew Burns), who had purchased the rights to two pieces of equipment called "Insta-machines", opened their first restaurants. Their production model was based on one of the machines they had acquired, an oven called the "Insta-Broiler". This strategy proved to be so successful that they later required all of their franchises to use the device.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> After the company faltered in 1959, it was purchased by its Miami, Florida, franchisees, James McLamore and David R. Edgerton. They initiated a corporate restructuring of the chain, first renaming the company Burger King. They ran the company as an independent entity for eight years (eventually expanding to over 250 locations in the United States), before selling it to the <a href="/wiki/Pillsbury_Company" title="Pillsbury Company">Pillsbury Company</a> in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>28</span></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_1969_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Burger_King_1969_logo.svg/200px-Burger_King_1969_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="189" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Burger_King_1969_logo.svg/300px-Burger_King_1969_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Burger_King_1969_logo.svg/400px-Burger_King_1969_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="208" data-file-height="197" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Logo from May 1, 1969 until April 30, 1994</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_1994_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Burger_King_1994_logo.svg/200px-Burger_King_1994_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Burger_King_1994_logo.svg/300px-Burger_King_1994_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Burger_King_1994_logo.svg/400px-Burger_King_1994_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Logo from May 1, 1994 until June 30, 1999</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg/220px-Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg/330px-Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg/440px-Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Big_King_XXL_Menu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burger King Big King XXL Meal</div></div></div>
<p>Pillsbury's management tried several times to restructure Burger King during the late 1970s and the early 1980s. The most prominent change came in 1978 when Burger King hired <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald&#39;s">McDonald's</a> executive <a href="/wiki/Donald_N._Smith" title="Donald N. Smith">Donald N. Smith</a> to help revamp the company. In a plan called "Operation Phoenix",<sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>118</span></sup> Smith restructured corporate business practices at all levels of the company. Changes included updated franchise agreements,<sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> a broader menu<sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>119</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>66</span></sup> and new standardized restaurant designs. Smith left Burger King for <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a> in 1980<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-siler_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-siler-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> shortly before a system-wide decline in sales.
</p><p>Pillsbury's Executive Vice President of Restaurant Operations <a href="/wiki/Norman_E._Brinker" title="Norman E. Brinker">Norman E. Brinker</a> was tasked with turning the brand around, and strengthening its position against its main rival McDonald's. One of his initiatives was a new advertising campaign featuring a series of <a href="/wiki/Attack_ad" title="Attack ad">attack ads</a> against its major competitors. This campaign started a competitive period between Burger King, McDonald's, and top burger chains known as the <a href="/wiki/Burger_wars" title="Burger wars">Burger wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nrn-brinker_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-brinker-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Brinker left Burger King in 1984, to take over Dallas-based gourmet burger chain <a href="/wiki/Chili%27s" title="Chili&#39;s">Chili's</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chili&#39;s_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chili&#39;s-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Smith and Brinker's efforts were initially effective,<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-smith_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-smith-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> but after their respective departures, Pillsbury relaxed or discarded many of their changes, and scaled back on construction of new locations. These actions stalled corporate growth and sales declined again, eventually resulting in a damaging fiscal slump for Burger King and Pillsbury.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-franchises_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-franchises-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dumaine_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumaine-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Poor operation and ineffectual leadership continued to bog down the company for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumaine_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumaine-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrn-ad_exec_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-ad_exec-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pillsbury was eventually acquired by the British entertainment conglomerate <a href="/wiki/Grand_Metropolitan" title="Grand Metropolitan">Grand Metropolitan</a> in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-minyanville_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minyanville-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Businessweek-3G_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Businessweek-3G-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Initially, Grand Met attempted to bring the chain to profitability under newly minted CEO Barry Gibbons; the changes he initiated during his two-year tenure had mixed results, as successful new product introductions and tie-ins with <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a> were offset by continuing image problems and ineffectual advertising programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Gibbons sold off several of the company's assets in attempt to profit from their sale and laid off many of its staff members.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-distron_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-distron-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrn-prewitt_distron_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-prewitt_distron-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrn-onex_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-onex-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Gibbon's departure, a series of CEOs each tried to repair the company's brand by changing the menu, bringing in new ad agencies and many other changes.<sup id="cite_ref-Stieghorst-Gibbons_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stieghorst-Gibbons-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt-gilpin_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-gilpin-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrn-nash&amp;lowes_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-nash&amp;lowes-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The parental disregard of the Burger King brand continued with Grand Metropolitan's merger with <a href="/wiki/Guinness" title="Guinness">Guinness</a> in 1997 when the two organizations formed the holding company <a href="/wiki/Diageo" title="Diageo">Diageo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Eventually, the ongoing systematic institutional neglect of the brand through a string of owners damaged the company to the point where major franchises were driven out of business, and its total value was significantly decreased.<sup id="cite_ref-Reese_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reese-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Diageo eventually decided to divest itself of the money-losing chain and put the company up for sale in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-times-batt_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times-batt-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt-sorkin_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-sorkin-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG/220px-Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG/330px-Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG/440px-Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4403" data-file-height="3451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger-King-Interior-Cork-Ireland-2012.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of the 20/20 concept interior at a Burger King in Cork, Ireland</div></div></div>
<p>The twenty-first century saw the company return to independence when it was purchased from Diageo by a group of investment firms led by <a href="/wiki/TPG_Capital" title="TPG Capital">TPG Capital</a> for US$1.5&#160;billion in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mark_Trumbull_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_Trumbull-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The new owners rapidly moved to revitalize and reorganize the company, culminating with the company being taken public in 2006 with a highly successful <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bkcnn_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bkcnn-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krantz-ipo_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krantz-ipo-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The firms' strategy for turning the chain around included a new advertising agency and new ad campaigns,<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz-Blum_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-Blum-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BKC-CPB_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BKC-CPB-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> a revamped menu strategy,<sup id="cite_ref-aa-superfan_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aa-superfan-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> a series of programs designed to revamp individual stores,<sup id="cite_ref-flex-1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flex-1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> a new restaurant concept called the <i><a href="/wiki/BK_Whopper_Bar" title="BK Whopper Bar">BK Whopper Bar</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday-2020_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday-2020-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and a new design format called <i>20/20</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday-2020_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday-2020-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> These changes successfully re-energized the company, leading to a score of profitable quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-Jargon-sales_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jargon-sales-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Yet, despite the successes of the new owners, the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">financial crisis of 2007–2010</a> weakened the company's financial outlooks while those of its immediate competitor McDonald's grew.<sup id="cite_ref-Jargon-sales_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jargon-sales-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters-3G_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-3G-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The falling value of Burger King eventually led to TPG and its partners divesting their interest in the chain in a US$3.26&#160;billion sale to 3G Capital of Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-Heher_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heher-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-BK_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-BK-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Analysts from financial firms <a href="/wiki/UBS" title="UBS">UBS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stifel_Nicolaus" class="mw-redirect" title="Stifel Nicolaus">Stifel Nicolaus</a> agreed that 3G would have to invest heavily in the company to help reverse its fortunes.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-BK_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-BK-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baertlein-3G_2_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baertlein-3G_2-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> After the deal was completed, the company's stock was removed from the New York Stock Exchange, ending a four-year period as a public company.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The delisting of its stock was designed to help the company repair its fundamental business structures and continue working to close the gap with McDonald's without having to worry about pleasing shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-Baertlein-3G_2_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baertlein-3G_2-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_domestic_market" title="United States domestic market">United States domestic market</a>, the chain has fallen to third place in terms of same store sales behind Ohio-based Wendy's. The decline is the result of 11 consecutive quarters of same store sales decline.<sup id="cite_ref-USAT_Reinvention_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAT_Reinvention-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2014, 3G announced that it planned to acquire the Canadian restaurant and coffee shop chain <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hortons" title="Tim Hortons">Tim Hortons</a> and merge it with Burger King with backing from <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a>. The two chains will retain separate operations post-merger, with Burger King remaining in its Miami headquarters.<sup id="cite_ref-cbc-acquireconfirmed_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc-acquireconfirmed-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> A Tim Hortons representative stated that the proposed merger would allow Tim Hortons to leverage Burger King's resources for international growth. The combined company will be the third-largest international chain of fast food restaurants.<sup id="cite_ref-gandm-bktimmies_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gandm-bktimmies-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-bktimmies_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-bktimmies-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The deal lead to a controversy over the practice of <a href="/wiki/Tax_inversion" title="Tax inversion">tax inversions</a>, in which a company decreases the amount of taxes it pays by moving its headquarters to a <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a>, a country with lower rates but maintains the majority of their operations in their previous location. As a high-profile instance of tax inversion, news of the merger was criticized by U.S. politicians, who felt that the move would result in a loss of tax revenue to foreign interests, and could result in further government pressure against inversions.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj-bktimmies_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-bktimmies-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lat-inversion_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lat-inversion-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-bkinversion_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-bkinversion-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt-BurgerKing_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-BurgerKing-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_operations">Structure and operations</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Burger King&#39;s corporate headquarters" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg/220px-Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg/330px-Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg/440px-Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2620" data-file-height="2083" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burgerkingheadquarters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Company headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated</a> <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">Miami-Dade County, Florida</a></div></div></div>
<p>Burger King Holdings is the parent company of Burger King, also known as Burger King Corporation and abbreviated BKC, and is a <a href="/wiki/Delaware_corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delaware corporation">Delaware corporation</a> formed on July 23, 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span></span></sup> A subsidiary, it derives its income from several sources, including property rental and sales through company owned restaurants;<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span></span></sup> however, a substantial portion of its revenue is dependent on franchise fees.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span></span></sup> During the transitional period after the acquisition of the company by <a href="/wiki/3G_Capital" title="3G Capital">3G Capital</a>, Burger King's board of directors was co-chaired by John W. Chidsey, formerly CEO and chairman of the company, and <a href="/wiki/Alex_Behring" title="Alex Behring">Alex Behring</a>, managing partner of 3G Capital.<sup id="cite_ref-Cordal_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cordal-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> By April 2011, the new ownership completed the restructuring of Burger King's corporate management and Chidsey tendered his resignation, leaving Behring as CEO and chair.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The company operates approximately 40 subsidiaries globally that oversee franchise operations, acquisitions and financial obligations such as pensions.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>Exhibit 21.1</span></sup> One example of a subsidiary is Burger King Brands, Inc. which is responsible for the management of Burger King's intellectual properties. A wholly owned subsidiary established in 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg-BKB_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-BKB-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Burger King Brands owns and manages all trademarks, copyrights and domain names used by the restaurants in the United States and Canada. It is also responsible for providing marketing and related services to the parent company.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, the majority of Burger King restaurants, approximately 90%, were privately held franchises.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-Rexrode_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-Rexrode-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In North America, Burger King Corporation is responsible for licensing operators and administering of stores. Internationally, the company often pairs with other parties to operate locations or it will outright sell the operational and administrative rights to a franchisee which is given the designation of <a href="/wiki/Master_franchise" title="Master franchise">master franchise</a> for the territory. The master franchise will then be expected to sub-license new stores, provide training support, and ensure operational standards are maintained. In exchange for the oversight responsibilities, the master franchise will receive administrative and advertising support from Burger King Corporation to ensure a common marketing scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-pdi-sanchez_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdi-sanchez-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NRN-Gale_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-Gale-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/3G_Capital" title="3G Capital">3G Capital</a> ownership group announced in April 2011 that it would begin divesting itself of many corporate owned locations with the intent to increase the number of privately held restaurants to 95%.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-Rexrode_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-Rexrode-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> As of 2016, the percentage of privately owned Burger King establishments grew to 99.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-largest_chains_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largest_chains-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the franchisor for the brand, Burger King Holdings has several obligations and responsibilities; the company designs and deploys corporate training systems while overseeing brand standards such as building design and appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-flex-1_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flex-1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFM-Maze_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFM-Maze-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Daszkowski_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daszkowski-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The company also develops new products and deploys them after presenting them to its franchises for approval per a 2010 agreement between itself and the franchise ownership groups.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-Rexrode_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-Rexrode-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Burger King has limited approval over franchise operations such as minimum hours of operation and promotional pricing.<sup id="cite_ref-CBS-Hours_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS-Hours-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MH-Hours_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MH-Hours-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Burger King designates approved vendors and distributors while ensuring safety standards at the productions facilities of its vendors.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span></span></sup>
</p><p>Burger King is headquartered in a nine-story office tower by the <a href="/wiki/Miami_International_Airport" title="Miami International Airport">Miami International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated</a> Miami-Dade County, Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Elaine Walker of the <i><a href="/wiki/Miami_Herald" title="Miami Herald">Miami Herald</a></i> stated that the headquarters has a "Burger King" sign that drivers on <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_836" title="Florida State Road 836">State Road 836</a> "can't miss". In addition, the chain planned to build a neon sign on the roof to advertise the brand to passengers landing at the airport. On Monday July 8, 2002, 130 employees began working at the Burger King headquarters with the remainder moving in phases in August 2002. Prior to the moving to its current headquarters in 2002, Burger King had considered moving away from the Miami area to Texas; Miami-Dade County politicians and leaders lobbied against this, and Burger King stayed.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In August 2014, the future of the company's Miami headquarters was again in doubt as reports surfaced that Burger King was in talks about buying the Canadian restaurant chain <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hortons" title="Tim Hortons">Tim Hortons</a>, with a view to relocating its headquarters to Canada where the corporate tax rate was lower.<sup id="cite_ref-HortonBK_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HortonBK-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The merger between Burger King and Tim Hortons created the fast food company now known as Restaurant Brands International Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The company's previous headquarters were in a southern Dade County campus located on Old Cutler Boulevard in the <a href="/wiki/Cutler,_Florida" title="Cutler, Florida">Cutler</a> <a href="/wiki/Census-designated_place" title="Census-designated place">census-designated place</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The former Burger King headquarters as of 2007 houses rental offices for several companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Bacardi_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bacardi-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Franchises">Franchises</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_franchises" title="Burger King franchises">Burger King franchises</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King in London, England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg/220px-Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg/330px-Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg/440px-Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leicester_Square_Burger_King.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burger King restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Leicester_Square" title="Leicester Square">Leicester Square</a>, London, England</div></div></div>
<p>When Burger King Corporation began franchising in 1959, it used a regional model where franchisees purchased the right to open stores within a geographic region.<sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>117</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Franchising" title="Franchising">franchise</a> agreements granted BKC very little oversight control of its franchisees and resulted in issues of product quality control, store image and design, and operational procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>118</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup>
</p><p>During the 1970s, structural deficiencies in Burger King's franchise system became increasingly problematic for Pillsbury. A major example was the relationship between Burger King and Louisiana-based franchisee Chart House,<sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup> Burger King's largest franchisee group at the time with over 350 locations in the United States. The company's owners, William and James Trotter, made several moves to take over or acquire Burger King during the 1970s, all of which were spurned by Pillsbury.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> After the failed attempts to acquire the company, the relationship between Chart House and Burger King soured and eventually devolved into a lawsuit.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Chart House eventually spun off its Burger King operations in the early 1980s into a holding company called DiversiFoods which, in turn, was acquired by Pillsbury in 1984 and absorbed into Burger King's operations.<sup id="cite_ref-trotters_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trotters-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of the franchising reorganization segment of Operation Phoenix, Donald N. Smith initiated a restructuring of future franchising agreements in 1978. Under this new franchise agreement, new owners were disallowed from living more than one hour from their restaurants – restricting them to smaller individuals or ownership groups and preventing large, multi-state corporations from owning franchises. Franchisees were also now prohibited from operating other chains, preventing them from diverting funds away from their Burger King holdings. This new policy effectively limited the size of franchisees and prevented larger franchises from challenging Burger King Corporation as Chart House had.<sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup> Smith also sought to have BKC be the primary owner of new locations and rent or lease the restaurants to its franchises. This policy would allow the company to take over the operations of failing stores or evict those owners who would not conform to the company guidelines and policies.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> By 1988, parent company <a href="/wiki/Pillsbury_Company" title="Pillsbury Company">Pillsbury</a> had relaxed many of Smith's changes, scaling back on construction of new locations, which resulted in stalled growth of the brand.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-franchises_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-franchises-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Neglect of Burger King by new owner <a href="/wiki/Grand_Metropolitan" title="Grand Metropolitan">Grand Metropolitan</a> and its successor <a href="/wiki/Diageo" title="Diageo">Diageo</a><sup id="cite_ref-Reese_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reese-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> further hurt the standing of the brand, causing significant financial damage to BK franchises and straining relations between the parties.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman-Forbes_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman-Forbes-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King in Oaxaca, Mexico" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/220px-Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/330px-Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/440px-Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3902" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Burger King franchise adapted to operate in the <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">historic district</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca_de_Juarez" class="mw-redirect" title="Oaxaca de Juarez">Oaxaca</a>, Mexico</div></div></div>
<p>By 2001 and after nearly 18 years of stagnant growth, the state of its franchises was beginning to affect the value of the company. One of the franchises most heavily affected by the lack of growth was the nearly 400-store AmeriKing Inc., one of the largest Burger King franchisees.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> By 2002, the franchise owner, which until this point had been struggling under a nearly US$300&#160;million debt load and been shedding stores across the US, was forced to enter <a href="/wiki/Chapter_11,_Title_11,_United_States_Code" title="Chapter 11, Title 11, United States Code">Chapter 11</a> bankruptcy.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-ameriking_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-ameriking-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The failure of AmeriKing deeply affected the value of Burger King, and put negotiations between Diaego and the TPC Capital-led group on hold. The developments eventually forced Diaego to lower the total selling price of the chain by almost <span class="nowrap">$750&#160;million</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman-Forbes_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman-Forbes-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> After the sale, newly appointed CEO <a href="/wiki/Brad_Blum" title="Brad Blum">Brad Blum</a> initiated a program to help roughly 20 percent of its franchises, including its four largest, who were in financial distress, bankruptcy or had ceased operations altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-krt1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krt1-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Partnering with California-based Trinity Capital, LLC, the company established the Franchisee Financial Restructuring Initiative, a program to address the financial issues facing BK's financially distressed franchisees. The initiative was designed to assist franchisees in restructuring their businesses to meet financial obligations, focus on restaurant operational excellence, reinvest in their operations, and return to profitability.<sup id="cite_ref-bison-bkcffri_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bison-bkcffri-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individual franchisees took advantage of the AmeriKing failure; one of BK's regional owners, Miami-based Al Cabrera, purchased 130 stores located primarily in the Chicago and the upper <a href="/wiki/Mid-west" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-west">mid-west</a> region, from the failed company for a price of <span class="nowrap">$16&#160;million</span>, approximately 88 percent of their original value. The new company, which started out as Core Value Partners and eventually became <a href="/wiki/Heartland_Foods" class="mw-redirect" title="Heartland Foods">Heartland Foods</a>, also purchased 120 additional stores from distressed owners and revamped them. The resulting purchases made Cabrera the largest minority franchisee of Burger King, and Heartland one of the company's top franchises.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> By 2006, the company was valued at over <span class="nowrap">$150&#160;million</span>, and was sold to New York–based <a href="/wiki/GSO_Capital_Partners" title="GSO Capital Partners">GSO Capital Partners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Other purchasers included a three-way group of <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a> athletes <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Faulk" title="Kevin Faulk">Kevin Faulk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Allen" title="Marcus Allen">Marcus Allen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Strahan" title="Michael Strahan">Michael Strahan</a> who collectively purchased 17 stores in the cities of <a href="/wiki/Norfolk,_Virginia" title="Norfolk, Virginia">Norfolk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>-based franchisee Dave Devoy, who purchased 32 AmeriKing stores. After investing in new decor, equipment and staff retraining, many of the formerly failing stores showed growth approaching 20 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Reese_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reese-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of 3G's restructuring plan, the company decided to divest itself of its corporate owned locations by re-franchising them to private owners and become a 100% franchised operation by the end of 2013. The project, which began in April 2012, saw the company divest corporate-owned locations in Florida, Canada, Spain, Germany, and other regions.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN-Divesture_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-Divesture-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NRN-Divestiture_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-Divestiture-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yahoo-Divesture_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo-Divesture-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The move gave the company a Q3, 2013 profit of US$68.2 million over the same quarter, 2012 of US$6.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN-Divesture_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-Divesture-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of its 2013 fiscal year, Burger King was the second largest <a href="/wiki/Chain_store" title="Chain store">chain</a> of hamburger fast food restaurants in terms of global locations,<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>123</span></sup> behind industry bellwether McDonald's, which had 32,400 locations. At the end of 2014, Burger King ranked 4th among US food chains in terms of US sales, behind <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald&#39;s">McDonald's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subway_(restaurant)" title="Subway (restaurant)">Subway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Burger King now has over 12,000 stores worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_operations">International operations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_with_Burger_King_franchises" title="List of countries with Burger King franchises">List of countries with Burger King franchises</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungry_Jack%27s" title="Hungry Jack&#39;s">Hungry Jack's</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Chalco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King restaurant in Mexico City" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Burger_King_Chalco.jpg/220px-Burger_King_Chalco.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Burger_King_Chalco.jpg/330px-Burger_King_Chalco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Burger_King_Chalco.jpg/440px-Burger_King_Chalco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Chalco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burger King located in <a href="/wiki/Chalco_de_D%C3%ADaz_Covarrubias" title="Chalco de Díaz Covarrubias">Chalco</a>, Mexico City</div></div></div>
<p>While BK began its foray into locations outside of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental United States">continental United States</a> in 1963 with a store in <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan, Puerto Rico</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bk-pr_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-pr-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> it did not have an international presence until several years later. Shortly after the acquisition of the chain by Pillsbury, it opened its first Canadian restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor, Ontario</a> in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>66</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Other international locations followed soon after: Oceania in 1971 and Europe in 1975 with a restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Madrid,_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrid, Spain">Madrid, Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH-BK_Australia_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-BK_Australia-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in 1982, BK and its franchisees began operating stores in several <a href="/wiki/East_Asian" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian">East Asian</a> countries, including Japan, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, Singapore and South Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Due to high competition, all of the Japanese locations were closed in 2001; however, BK reentered the Japanese market in June 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> BK's <a href="/wiki/Central_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American">Central</a> and South American operations began in Mexico in the late 1970s and by the early 1980s in <a href="/wiki/Caracas,_Venezuela" class="mw-redirect" title="Caracas, Venezuela">Caracas, Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santiago,_Chile" class="mw-redirect" title="Santiago, Chile">Santiago, Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires,_Argentina" class="mw-redirect" title="Buenos Aires, Argentina">Buenos Aires, Argentina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> While Burger King lags behind McDonald's in international locations by over 12,000 stores, as of 2008 it had managed to become the largest chain in several countries including Mexico and Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Emory-Chidsey_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emory-Chidsey-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The company divides its international operations into three segments; the Middle East, Europe and Africa division (EMEA), Asia-Pacific (APAC) and Latin America and the Caribbean (LAC).<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> In each of these regions, Burger King has established several subsidiaries to develop strategic partnerships and alliances to expand into new territories. In its EMEA group, Burger King's Switzerland-based subsidiary Burger King Europe GmbH is responsible for the licensing and development of BK franchises in those regions.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5, Exhibit 21:1</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In APAC region, the Singapore-based BK AsiaPac, Pte. Ltd. business unit handles franchising for East Asia, the Asian subcontinent and all Oceanic territories.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6, Exhibit 21:1</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pdi-sanchez_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pdi-sanchez-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The LAC region includes Mexico, Central and South America and the Caribbean Islands and has no centralized operations group.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6, Exhibit 21:1</span></sup>
</p><p>Australia is the only country in which Burger King does not operate under its own name.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_10-K_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_10-K-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> When the company set about establishing operations down under in 1971, it found that its business name was already trademarked by a takeaway food shop in <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-terry-forrest_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terry-forrest-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Burger King provided the Australian franchisee, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Cowin" title="Jack Cowin">Jack Cowin</a>, with a list of possible alternative names derived from pre-existing trademarks already registered by Burger King and its then corporate parent Pillsbury, that could be used to name the Australian restaurants. Cowin selected the "Hungry Jack" brand name, one of Pillsbury's US pancake mixture products, and slightly changed the name to a possessive form by adding an <a href="/wiki/Possessive_apostrophe" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive apostrophe">apostrophe</a> "s" forming the new name Hungry Jack's.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH-BK_Australia_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-BK_Australia-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BK_Australia_2_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK_Australia_2-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> After the expiration of the trademark in the late 1990s, Burger King unsuccessfully tried to introduce the brand to the continent. After losing a lawsuit filed against it by Hungry Jack's ownership, the company ceded the territory to its franchisee.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH-BK_Australia_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-BK_Australia-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Hungry Jack's is now the only Burger King brand in Australia; Cowin's company Hungry Jack's PTY is the <a href="/wiki/Master_franchise" title="Master franchise">master franchise</a> and thus is now responsible for oversight of the operations that country with Burger King only providing administrative and advertising support to ensure a common marketing scheme for the company and its products.<sup id="cite_ref-NRN-Gale_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-Gale-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Beijing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King in Beijing, China" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Burger_King_Beijing.jpg/220px-Burger_King_Beijing.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Burger_King_Beijing.jpg/330px-Burger_King_Beijing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Burger_King_Beijing.jpg/440px-Burger_King_Beijing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1205" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Beijing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burger King in <a href="/wiki/Beijing_Capital_International_Airport" title="Beijing Capital International Airport">Beijing International Airport</a>, Beijing, China</div></div></div>
<p>Over a 10-year period starting in 2008, Burger King predicted 80 percent of its market share would be driven by foreign expansion, particularly in the Asia-Pacific and <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> regional markets.<sup id="cite_ref-EM_Becker_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EM_Becker-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> While the TPG-led group continued BK's international expansion by announcing plans to open new franchise locations in Eastern Europe, Africa and the Middle East, and Brazil, the company plan is focusing on the three largest markets – India, China, and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-BKPR-Poland_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BKPR-Poland-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SPTimes-BK_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPTimes-BK-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BK-Hana_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK-Hana-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BK-Egypt_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK-Egypt-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The company plans to add over 250 stores in these Asian territories, as well as other places such as <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, by the end of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg-Asia_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-Asia-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Its expansion into the Indian market has the company at a competitive disadvantage with other <a href="/wiki/Fast_food_restaurant" title="Fast food restaurant">fast food restaurants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/KFC" title="KFC">KFC</a> because of the aversion of the country's large <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> majority to beef. BK hopes to use their non-beef products, such as their <a href="/wiki/List_of_Burger_King_products#TenderCrisp" title="List of Burger King products">TenderCrisp</a> and <a href="/wiki/BK_grilled_chicken_sandwiches" class="mw-redirect" title="BK grilled chicken sandwiches">TenderGrill</a> chicken sandwiches, as well as other products like mutton sandwiches and veggie sandwiches, to help them overcome this hurdle to expand in that country.<sup id="cite_ref-EM_Becker_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EM_Becker-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters-HT_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-HT-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> 3G has reported that it will continue with the plans to grow globally, even ramping up the planned expansion to help increase their return on investment.<sup id="cite_ref-Businessweek-3G_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Businessweek-3G-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup> It is expected that 3G Brazilian-based management connections in the region may help Burger King expand in Brazil and Latin America, where it has been having problems finding acceptable franchisees.<sup id="cite_ref-Businessweek-3G_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Businessweek-3G-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-3G_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-3G-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_legal_cases">Controversies and legal cases</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_legal_issues" title="Burger King legal issues">Burger King legal issues</a>; <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_(Mattoon,_Illinois)" title="Burger King (Mattoon, Illinois)">Burger King (Mattoon, Illinois)</a>; <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_(Alberta)" title="Burger King (Alberta)">Burger King (Alberta)</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_Corporation_v_Hungry_Jack%27s_Pty_Ltd" title="Burger King Corporation v Hungry Jack&#39;s Pty Ltd">Burger King Corporation v Hungry Jack's Pty Ltd</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The Hoot&#39;s family Burger King in Mattoon, Illinois, unrelated to Burger King Corporation" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg/220px-Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg/330px-Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg/440px-Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mattoon_Burger_King.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Burger King</i> restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Mattoon,_Illinois" title="Mattoon, Illinois">Mattoon, Illinois</a>, originally owned by the Hoots family. This location was one subject of major litigation by Burger King.</div></div></div>
<p>Burger King has been involved in several legal disputes and cases, as both plaintiff and defendant, in the years since its founding in 1954. Depending on the ownership and executive staff at the time of these incidents, the company's responses to these challenges have ranged from a conciliatory dialog with its critics and litigants, to a more aggressive opposition with questionable tactics and negative consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-nyhc_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-nyhc-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fmnp_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fmnp-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fmnp-spy_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fmnp-spy-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nrn-ciw_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-ciw-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The company's response to these various issues has drawn praise<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-PETA_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-PETA-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox-PETA_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-PETA-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> as well as accusations of political appeasement from different parties over the years.<sup id="cite_ref-otb_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-otb-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A trademark dispute involving the owners of the identically named Burger King in <a href="/wiki/Mattoon,_Illinois" title="Mattoon, Illinois">Mattoon, Illinois</a>, led to a federal lawsuit. The case's outcome helped define the scope of the <a href="/wiki/Lanham_act" class="mw-redirect" title="Lanham act">Lanham act</a> and <a href="/wiki/US_trademark_law" class="mw-redirect" title="US trademark law">trademark law</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-hoots_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoots-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> An existing trademark held by a shop of the same name in South Australia forced the company to change its name in Australia,<sup id="cite_ref-BKau_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BKau-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> while another state trademark in Texas forced the company to abandon its signature product, the Whopper, in several counties around San Antonio.<sup id="cite_ref-barkoff_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barkoff-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The company was only able to enter northern <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, in 1995, after it paid the founders of <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_(Alberta)" title="Burger King (Alberta)">another chain named Burger King</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alberta_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alberta-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Legal decisions from other suits have set contractual law precedents in regards to <a href="/wiki/Long-arm_statute" class="mw-redirect" title="Long-arm statute">long-arm statutes</a>, the limitations of <a href="/wiki/Franchising" title="Franchising">franchise agreements</a>, and ethical business practices.<sup id="cite_ref-findlaw_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findlaw-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-teply_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-teply-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Many of these decisions have helped define general business dealings that continue to shape the entire marketplace.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-temple-lee_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-temple-lee-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fls-Welkowitz_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fls-Welkowitz-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Controversies and disputes have arisen with groups such as <a href="/wiki/People_for_the_Ethical_Treatment_of_Animals" title="People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals">People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals</a> (PETA), governmental and social agencies, and unions and trade groups over various topics. These situations have touched on legal and moral concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_responsibility" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporate responsibility">corporate responsibility</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ctv_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Business_ethics" title="Business ethics">ethics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-BK_accedes_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-BK_accedes-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-BK_accedes_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-BK_accedes-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> While the majority of the disputes did not result in lawsuits, in many of the cases, the situations raised legal questions, dealt with legal compliance, or resulted in legal remedies such as changes in <a href="/wiki/Contract_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Contract law">contractual procedure</a> or binding agreements between parties. The resolutions to these legal matters have often altered the way the company interacts and negotiates contracts with its suppliers and franchisees, or how it does business with the public.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-PETA_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-PETA-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox-PETA_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-PETA-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scheck_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scheck-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-holton_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holton-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further controversies have occurred during the company's expansion in the Middle East. The opening of a Burger King location in <a href="/wiki/Ma%27aleh_Adumim" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma&#39;aleh Adumim">Ma'aleh Adumim</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Israeli_settlement" title="Israeli settlement">Israeli settlement</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Israeli-occupied_territories" title="Israeli-occupied territories">Israeli-occupied</a> <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestinian territories</a>, led to a <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_contract" title="Breach of contract">breach of contract</a> dispute between Burger King and its Israeli franchise due to the hotly contested international dispute over the legality of Israeli settlements in the Palestinian territories in accordance to international law. The controversy eventually erupted into a geopolitical dispute involving Muslim and Jewish groups on multiple continents over the application of, and adherence to, <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jewishhsf_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jewishhsf-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Faxx-Jewish_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faxx-Jewish-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Faxx-Muslim_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faxx-Muslim-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The case eventually elicited reactions from the members of the 22-nation <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic countries">Islamic countries</a> within the League made a joint threat to the company of legal sanctions including the revocation of Burger King's <a href="/wiki/Business_license" title="Business license">business licenses</a> within the member states' territories.<sup id="cite_ref-Faxx-Jewish_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faxx-Jewish-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Faxx-Muslim_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faxx-Muslim-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-muddle_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muddle-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A related issue involving members of the Islamic faith over the interpretation of the Muslim version of <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shariah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shariah">Shariah</a>, regarding the promotional artwork on a dessert package in the United Kingdom raised issues of cultural sensitivity,<sup id="cite_ref-scotsman_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotsman-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and, with the former example, posed a larger question about the lengths that companies must go to, to ensure the smooth operation of their businesses in the communities they serve.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-rage_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-rage-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>On April 9, 2019 <a href="/wiki/Nations_Restaurant_News" class="mw-redirect" title="Nations Restaurant News">Nations Restaurant News</a> reported that Burger King filed a lawsuit on Fritz Management LLC  to remove Burger King trademarks from 37 units in <a href="/wiki/South_Texas" title="South Texas">South Texas</a> after unsanitary conditions were found at a restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Harlingen,_Texas" title="Harlingen, Texas">Harlingen Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In May 2019, the lawsuit was settled with the franchisee, Fritz Management (a subsidiary of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sun_Holdings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sun Holdings (page does not exist)">Sun Holdings</a> Inc) keeping the trademarks on all 37 units.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charitable_contributions_and_services">Charitable contributions and services</span></h2>
<p>Burger King has two of its own in-house national charitable organizations and programs. One is the Have It Your Way Foundation, a US-based non-profit (<a href="/wiki/501(c)(3)" class="mw-redirect" title="501(c)(3)">501(c)(3)</a>) corporation with multiple focuses on hunger alleviation, disease prevention and community education through scholarship programs at colleges in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-bk-hiyw_foundation_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-hiyw_foundation-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The other charitable organization is the McLamore Foundation, also a non-profit, 501(c)(3) corporation that provides scholarships to students in the US and its territories.<sup id="cite_ref-bk-mclamore_foundation_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-mclamore_foundation-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In various regions across the United States, Burger King and its franchises have aligned themselves with several charitable organizations that support research and treatment of juvenile cancer. Each year, these coalitions hold a fund raising drive called "A Chance for Kids", in which Burger King restaurants sell <a href="/wiki/Lottery" title="Lottery">lottery</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Scratch_cards" class="mw-redirect" title="Scratch cards">scratch cards</a> for $1. Each card produces a winning prize that is usually a food or beverage product, but includes (rarer) items such as shopping sprees or trips. In the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, BK has affiliated itself with the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> team the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> and its charitable foundation, the <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="Jimmy Fund">Jimmy Fund</a>. The group runs the contest in Boston. In the New York City area, it operates the contest in association with the Burger King Children's Charities of Metro New York and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>. Funds raised in these areas go to support the <a href="/wiki/Dana-Farber_Cancer_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Dana-Farber Cancer Institute">Dana-Farber Cancer Institute</a>, located in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-jf-chfk_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jf-chfk-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jf-nyc_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jf-nyc-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In Nebraska, the company is affiliated with the Liz's Legacy Cancer Fund "BK Beat Cancer for Kids" program at the UNMC Eppley Cancer Center at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nebraska_Medical_Center" title="University of Nebraska Medical Center">University of Nebraska Medical Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Omaha,_Nebraska" title="Omaha, Nebraska">Omaha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bk-bcfk_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-bcfk-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> region, it funded the establishment of the Burger King Cancer Caring Center, a support organization for the families and friends of cancer patients.<sup id="cite_ref-bk-cc_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-cc-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_products" title="Burger King products">Burger King products</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Burger_King_products" title="List of Burger King products">List of Burger King products</a></div>
