<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_king_whopper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Whopper sandwich" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Burger_king_whopper.jpg/220px-Burger_king_whopper.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Burger_king_whopper.jpg/330px-Burger_king_whopper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Burger_king_whopper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_king_whopper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Whopper" title="Whopper">Whopper</a> sandwich, Burger King's signature product</div></div></div>
<p>When the predecessor of Burger King first opened in Jacksonville in 1953, its menu consisted predominantly of basic hamburgers, French fries, soft drinks, milkshakes, and desserts. After being acquired by its Miami, Florida, franchisees and renamed to its current moniker in 1954, BK began expanding the breadth of its menu by adding the Whopper sandwich in 1957. This quarter-pound (4&#160;oz (110&#160;g)) hamburger was created by Burger King's new owners <a href="/wiki/James_McLamore" title="James McLamore">James McLamore</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Edgerton" title="David Edgerton">David Edgerton</a> as a way to differentiate BK from other burger outlets at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-NRN-McLamore_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-McLamore-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Since its inception, the Whopper has become synonymous with Burger King and has become the focus of much of its advertising.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj-whopper_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-whopper-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The company even named its new kiosk-style restaurants <a href="/wiki/BK_Whopper_Bar" title="BK Whopper Bar">Whopper Bars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ap-whopper_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap-whopper-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The menu component of Donald Smith's Operation Phoenix was initiated in 1978 and led to the addition of the Burger King Specialty Sandwich line in 1979. The new product line significantly expanded the breadth of the BK menu with many non-hamburger sandwiches, including new chicken and fish offerings. The new Specialty Sandwich line was one of the first attempts to target a specific demographic, in this case, adults 18–34, who would be willing to spend more on a higher quality product.<sup id="cite_ref-Jakle_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jakle-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>119</span></sup> One of Smith's other significant contributions to the menu was the addition of a breakfast product line, which until this time was not a market Burger King had entered.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Besides the addition of the <a href="/wiki/Croissan%27Wich" class="mw-redirect" title="Croissan&#39;Wich">Croissan'Wich</a> in 1983, the breakfast menu remained almost identical to the McDonald's offerings until a menu revamp in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This expansion introduced BK's "Am Express" product line, which added new products such as <a href="/wiki/French_toast" title="French toast">French toast</a> sticks and <a href="/wiki/Muffin" title="Muffin">mini-muffins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nrn-fts_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-fts-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the company expanded both inside and outside the US, it introduced <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">localized versions</a> of its products that conform to regional tastes and cultural or religious beliefs. International variations add ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Teriyaki" title="Teriyaki">teriyaki</a> or <a href="/wiki/Beetroot" title="Beetroot">beetroot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fried_egg" title="Fried egg">fried egg</a> to the Whopper;<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> beer in Germany, Italy, and Spain; and <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kosher_foods" title="Kosher foods">kosher</a> products in the Middle East and Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-halal_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halal-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kosher_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kosher-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> To generate additional sales, BK will occasionally introduce limited time offers (LTOs) that are versions of its core products, or new products intended for either long or short term sales. Items such as the Texas Double Whopper and various sandwiches made with <a href="/wiki/Mushrooms" class="mw-redirect" title="Mushrooms">mushrooms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swiss_cheese" title="Swiss cheese">Swiss cheese</a> have been rotated in and out of its menu for several years,<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Time-manthem_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Time-manthem-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> while products such as its 1993 <a href="/wiki/Meatloaf" title="Meatloaf">Meatloaf</a> <a href="/wiki/Original_Chicken_Sandwich" class="mw-redirect" title="Original Chicken Sandwich">Specialty Sandwich</a> offering and accompanying limited <a href="/wiki/Table_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Table service">table service</a>, along with special dinner platters, failed to generate interest and were discontinued.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BurgerKingFood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King value meal" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/BurgerKingFood.jpg/220px-BurgerKingFood.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/BurgerKingFood.jpg/330px-BurgerKingFood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/BurgerKingFood.jpg/440px-BurgerKingFood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BurgerKingFood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A meal including small <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French fries</a>, a Whopper Jr., a drink, and packets of <a href="/wiki/H._J._Heinz_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="H. J. Heinz Company">Heinz</a> <a href="/wiki/Ketchup" title="Ketchup">ketchup</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In order to appeal to as many demographic groups as possible and better compete with its competitor <a href="/wiki/Wendy%27s" title="Wendy&#39;s">Wendy's</a>, Burger King added a multi-tiered <a href="/wiki/Value_menu" title="Value menu">value menu</a> in 1993 with items priced at 99¢, US$1.99 and $2.99.<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The additions, part of then CEO James Adamson's back to basics program also called Operation Phoenix, were an attempt to add not only a value menu, but also a line of <a href="/wiki/Value_meal" title="Value meal">value meals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nrn-vm_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrn-vm-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The tiered menu was replaced with a more standard value menu in 1998 while the value meals were separated into their own menu segment.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> This value menu featured seven products: Whopper Jr., five-piece Chicken Tenders, a bacon cheeseburger, medium-sized French fries, medium soft drink, medium onion rings, and small shake. In 2002 and 2006, BK revamped its value menu, adding and removing several different products such as <a href="/wiki/Chili_con_carne" title="Chili con carne">chili</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Rodeo_Cheeseburger" class="mw-redirect" title="Rodeo Cheeseburger">Rodeo Cheeseburger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Many of these items have since been discontinued, modified or relegated to a regional menu option.<sup id="cite_ref-BK-US_nutrition_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK-US_nutrition-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> To better appeal to a more adult palate and demographic, BK introduced several new products to its menu in 2003, including several new or revamped chicken products, a new salad line and its BK Joe brand of coffee. Some of the new products, including its <a href="/wiki/Enormous_Omelet_Sandwich" class="mw-redirect" title="Enormous Omelet Sandwich">Enormous Omelet Sandwich</a> line and the <a href="/wiki/BK_Stacker" title="BK Stacker">BK Stacker</a> line, brought negative attention due to the large portion size, and amounts of unhealthy fats and trans-fats.<sup id="cite_ref-komo_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-komo-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usat_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usat-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sr_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sr-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Many of these products featured higher quality ingredients like whole chicken breast, Angus beef, and natural cheeses such as <a href="/wiki/Cheddar_cheese" title="Cheddar cheese">cheddar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pepperjack" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepperjack">pepper jack</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Again, not all these products, such as the <a href="/wiki/BK_Baguette_line" class="mw-redirect" title="BK Baguette line">BK Baguette line</a>, have met corporate sales expectations.<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz-Blum_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-Blum-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the purchase of the company in 2010, 3G began a program to restructure its menu designed to move away from the male-oriented menu that had dominated under the previous ownership. The first major item to be introduced was a reformulation of its <a href="/wiki/BK_Chicken_Tenders" class="mw-redirect" title="BK Chicken Tenders">BK Chicken Tenders</a> product in March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-NRN_Revamped_tenders_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN_Revamped_tenders-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Over the next few months, approximately 20 new products were researched and developed while others were reformulated, including its Chef's Choice Burger.<sup id="cite_ref-UDH_Chef&#39;s_Choice_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UDH_Chef&#39;s_Choice-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Eventually pruned down to 10 items, Burger King began deploying the items in the United States throughout 2011–2012 with the official roll out beginning April 2012. The changes included new ice cream products, <a href="/wiki/Smoothies" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoothies">smoothies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frapp%C3%A9_coffee" title="Frappé coffee">frappés</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicken_strips" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken strips">chicken strips</a>. The Whopper was the most prominently reformulated product in this round of introductions with a new type of cheese and packaging.<sup id="cite_ref-USAT_Reinvention_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAT_Reinvention-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of 2015, Burger King's parent company, Restaurant Brands International, announced that none of its subsidiaries would use chicken that had been fed antibiotics that are "critically important" to human health; that announcement referred only to a small class of antibiotics for which there is only one drug that kill a kind of bacteria and the announcement was described as a "small step" by advocates for stopping all <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_use_in_livestock" title="Antibiotic use in livestock">antibiotic use in livestock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, Burger King announced plans to release an "Impossible Whopper" burger, a vegetarian burger using a plant-based patty from <a href="/wiki/Impossible_Foods" title="Impossible Foods">Impossible Foods</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An Burger King kitchen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg/220px-Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg/330px-Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg/440px-Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burger_King_Italy_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Food being prepared in a Burger King kitchen in Italy</div></div></div>
<p>Like its menu, the equipment the company cooks its hamburgers with has also evolved as the company expanded. The burgers have always been broiled mechanically; the original unit, called an Insta-Broiler, was one of two pieces of equipment the founders of Insta-Burger King purchased before opening their new restaurant.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>27</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NRN-McLamore_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-McLamore-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The Insta-Broiler worked by cooking 12 burger patties in a wire basket, allowing the patties to be cooked from both sides simultaneously.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>27</span></sup> When McLamore and Edgerton took over the company, besides dropping the "Insta-" prefix, they switched to an improved unit which they called a "Flame Broiler". Designed by the two and featuring stationary burners that cooked the meat on a moving chain, the unit broke down less often while maintaining a similar cooking rate.<sup id="cite_ref-NRN-McLamore_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRN-McLamore-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The company would stay with that format for the next 40 years until Burger King began developing a variable speed broiler that could handle multiple items with different cooking rates and times.<sup id="cite_ref-qsrw-broiler_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qsrw-broiler-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cebrzynski_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cebrzynski-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> These new units began testing in 1999 and eventually evolved into the two models the company deployed system-wide in 2008–2009. Accompanying these new broilers was new food-holding equipment, accompanied with a computer-based product monitoring system for its cooked products.<sup id="cite_ref-wbt_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wbt-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The monitoring system allows for more concise tracking of product quality while giving the company and its franchisees a method to streamline costs by more precisely projecting sales and product usage.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn-fm_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-fm-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_advertising" title="Burger King advertising">Burger King advertising</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Burger_King_ad_programs" title="List of Burger King ad programs">list of Burger King ad programs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nick-van-eede-KING-CROWN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Burger King crown on Nick Van Eede" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Nick-van-eede-KING-CROWN.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="236" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="207" data-file-height="236" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nick-van-eede-KING-CROWN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Burger King "crown", worn by <a href="/wiki/Nick_Van_Eede" title="Nick Van Eede">Nick Van Eede</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since its foundation in 1954, Burger King has employed varied advertising programs, both successful and unsuccessful. During the 1970s, output included its "Hold the pickles, hold the lettuce..." jingle, the inspiration for its current mascot <a href="/wiki/The_Burger_King" title="The Burger King">the Burger King</a>, and several well known and parodied <a href="/wiki/Slogans" class="mw-redirect" title="Slogans">slogans</a> such as "Have it your way" and "It takes two hands to handle a Whopper".<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bkcmah_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bkcmah-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sheph_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sheph-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Burger King introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Attack_ad" title="Attack ad">attack ad</a> in the fast food industry with a pre-teen <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Michelle_Gellar" title="Sarah Michelle Gellar">Sarah Michelle Gellar</a> in 1981. The television spot, which claimed BK burgers were larger and better tasting than competitor McDonald's,<sup id="cite_ref-Reiter_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reiter-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>66</span></sup> so enraged executives at McDonald's parent company that they sued all parties involved.<sup id="cite_ref-sheknows-gellar_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sheknows-gellar-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Starting in the early 1980s and running through approximately 2001, BK engaged a series of <a href="/wiki/Advertising_agency" title="Advertising agency">ad agencies</a> that produced many unsuccessful slogans and programs, including its biggest advertising flop "<a href="/wiki/Where%27s_Herb%3F" title="Where&#39;s Herb?">Where's Herb?</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-time_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Burger King was a pioneer in the advertising practice known as the "<a href="/wiki/Product_tie-in" class="mw-redirect" title="Product tie-in">product tie-in</a>", with a successful partnership with <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a>' <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">Lucasfilm</a>, Ltd., to promote the 1977 film <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i> in which BK sold a set of beverage glasses featuring the main characters from the movie.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> This promotion was one of the first in the <a href="/wiki/Fast_food_restaurant" title="Fast food restaurant">fast food</a> industry and set the pattern that continues to the present. BK's early success in the field was overshadowed by a 1982 deal between McDonald's and the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney Company">Walt Disney Company</a> to promote Disney's animated films beginning in the mid-1980s and running through the early 1990s. In 1994, Disney switched from McDonald's to Burger King, signing a 10-movie promotional contract which would include such top 10 films as <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i> (1992), <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> (1991), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i> (1994), and <i><a href="/wiki/Toy_Story" title="Toy Story">Toy Story</a></i> (1995).<sup id="cite_ref-Gale_20-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gale-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A partnership in association with the <a href="/wiki/Pok%C3%A9mon" title="Pokémon">Pokémon</a> franchise at the height of its popularity in 1999 was tremendously successful for the company, with many locations rapidly selling out of the toys and the replacements.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after the acquisition of Burger King by <a href="/wiki/TPG_Capital,_L.P." class="mw-redirect" title="TPG Capital, L.P.">TPG Capital, L.P.</a> in 2002, its new CEO Brad Blum set about turning around the fortunes of the company by initiating an overhaul of its flailing advertising programs. One of the first moves by the company was to reinstate its famous "Have it your way" slogan as the corporate motto. BK handed the effort off to its new advertising agency, Miami-based <a href="/wiki/Crispin_Porter_%2B_Bogusky" title="Crispin Porter + Bogusky">Crispin Porter + Bogusky</a> (abbreviated as CP+B). CP+B was known for having a hip, subversive tack when creating campaigns for its clients, exactly what BK was looking for.<sup id="cite_ref-Horovitz-Blum_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horovitz-Blum-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BKC-CPB_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BKC-CPB-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> One of CP+B strategies was to revive the Burger King character used during BK's 1970s/1980s <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_Kingdom" title="Burger King Kingdom">Burger King Kingdom</a> children's advertising campaign as a <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricatured</a> variation, now simply called "the King".<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-heavyweights_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heavyweights-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The farcical nature of "the Burger King" centered advertisements inspired an <a href="/wiki/Internet_meme" title="Internet meme">internet meme</a> where the King is <a href="/wiki/Photo_manipulation" title="Photo manipulation">edited</a> into unusual situations that are either comical or menacing, many times followed with the phrase "Where is your God now?"
</p><p>Additionally, CP+B created a series of new characters like <a href="/wiki/The_Subservient_Chicken" title="The Subservient Chicken">the Subservient Chicken</a> and the faux <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">nu metal</a> band <a href="/wiki/Coq_Roq" class="mw-redirect" title="Coq Roq">Coq Roq</a>, featured in a series of <a href="/wiki/Viral_marketing" title="Viral marketing">viral</a> web-based advertisements on sites such as MySpace and various BK corporate pages, to complement various television and print promotional campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-bk-my_space_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-my_space-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bk-table_guests_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bk-table_guests-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> One of the more successful promotions that CP+B devised was the creation of a series of three <a href="/wiki/Advergaming" class="mw-redirect" title="Advergaming">advergames</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ederly-Mollick_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ederly-Mollick-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wilson-Gamespot_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-Gamespot-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Created by UK-based <a href="/wiki/Blitz_Games" title="Blitz Games">Blitz Games</a> and featuring company celebrity spokesman <a href="/wiki/Brooke_Burke" title="Brooke Burke">Brooke Burke</a>, the games sold more than 3.2&#160;million copies, placing them as one of the top selling games along with another Xbox 360 hit, <i><a href="/wiki/Gears_of_War" title="Gears of War">Gears of War</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson-Gamespot_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-Gamespot-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BK-X-Box_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK-X-Box-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> These ad campaigns, coupled with other new promotions and a series of <a href="/wiki/Burger_King_products" title="Burger King products">new product</a> introductions, drew positive and negative attention to BK and helped TPG and its partners realize about US$367&#160;million in dividends.<sup id="cite_ref-BK-CPG_ads_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BK-CPG_ads-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s recession">late-2000s recession</a> hitting the 18–35 demographic targeted by the CP+B created ads particularly hard, the company saw its market share decline and the company move into the red. After the completion of the sale of the company in late 2010, the new ownership group terminated Burger King's seven-year relationship with CP+B and hired rival firm <a href="/wiki/McGarryBowen" title="McGarryBowen">McGarryBowen</a> to create a new campaign with an expanded market reach.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> As part of the new campaign, McGarryBowen terminated the use of <a href="/wiki/The_Burger_King" title="The Burger King">The Burger King</a> in the company's advertising program in favor of a new program that focused on the food and ingredients in its new advertising campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-AdAge-McGarryBowen_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdAge-McGarryBowen-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent years, Burger King has turned to <a href="/wiki/Trolling" class="mw-redirect" title="Trolling">trolling</a> fast food rival <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald&#39;s">McDonald's</a> with their advertising strategy. The company's tactics have included LOLA MullenLowe's "Scary Clown Night" which offered a free Whopper to anyone dressed as a clown (McDonald's mascot) on Halloween; FCB New York's Whopper Detour initiative, which encouraged mobile app users to go to a nearby McDonald's in order to unlock a 1-center Whopper; and Ingo's "The Not Big Macs" menu, which poked fun at McDonald's recent loss of the Big Mac trademark in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2019, the company launched an advertising campaign called "Eat Like Andy". The television spot which premiered during the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_LIII" title="Super Bowl LIII">Super Bowl LIII</a> features archival documentary film footage from "<a href="/wiki/66_Scenes_from_America" title="66 Scenes from America">66 Scenes from America</a>" by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgen_Leth" title="Jørgen Leth">Jørgen Leth</a> of the pop artist <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a> (1928-1987) unwrapping and eating a <a href="/wiki/Whopper" title="Whopper">Whopper</a>. The footage was approved for use by the fast food giant courtesy of the <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Andy Warhol Foundation">Andy Warhol Foundation</a>. Meanwhile, prior to the game, the mass market hamburger chain made available to viewers who ordered it in advance via <a href="/wiki/DoorDash" title="DoorDash">DoorDash</a> an "Andy Warhol Mystery Box" which with contains among other items a plastic bottle of ketchup and a platinum wig so one can "Eat Like Andy".<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
